Looking at this endgame position, I have a decisive material advantage with King + Queen + Pawn against White's lone King. The White King is currently at b2.

The key technique in K+Q vs K endgames is to restrict the opponent's king to a smaller "box" by positioning your Queen a knight's move away from the enemy King. This prevents the King from escaping while you gradually force it toward the edge of the board.

From h1, the move **d1** places the Queen exactly a knight's move away from the White King on b2 (2 files left, 1 rank up). This cuts off escape squares a1, b1, c1, and c2, confining the White King to the limited area of a2-a3-b2-b3-c2-c3.

This is much more precise than checking moves like Qb7+ which would allow the King to escape to various squares on the a and c-files.

**make_move h1d1**

Let me check my options:
- Promoting to Q/R/B/N on f1 all result in the piece being lost to Kxf1, except that Q and B promotions give check (but still lose the piece)
- The f2 pawn cannot be defended by the black king (Kg4 cannot reach f3 or g3 due to the white king controlling those squares and general king proximity rules)
- Other moves (like pushing other pawns or moving the king) allow White to simply capture the f2 pawn with Kxf2

Despite losing the queen immediately, promoting is actually the best practical chance because:
1. It gives a check (f2-f1=Q+), forcing White to respond
2. After Kxf1, White's king is stuck in the corner on f1, far less active than on g2 or f2
3. Black preserves the tempo and keeps the other pawns

While underpromoting to a bishop also gives check, a queen is the strongest piece and gives White the most chances to go wrong.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at this position, Black has a critical pawn on f2 ready to promote. Let me analyze the options:

**Key observation**: The f2 pawn can promote to f1. If I promote to a queen with check (f2-f1=Q+), White has two responses:
1. **Kxf1** - Captures the queen, but traps the White king in the corner on f1
2. **Kh2** - Escapes the check, but Black keeps the new queen

Since White will choose Kh2 to avoid immediate material loss, Black ends up with a decisive material advantage (Queen vs. minor pieces and pawns).

Other options are inferior:
- Not promoting allows Kxf2 losing the pawn
- Promoting to a knight (f2-f1=N+) blocks the Kh2 escape but wins less material
- Moving the king loses the f2 pawn

**Decision**: Promote to queen, forcing White to retreat to h2 or capture and get stuck in the corner.

make_move f2f1q

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at the board, I see that Black has a significant material advantage with a Queen against White's Knight and Pawn. The White King is exposed on c3, trapped by its own Knight on b2 (which ironically blocks the King's escape squares but also blocks Black's immediate checking opportunities).

Let me analyze the checking possibilities:
- **Qc2+** loses immediately to Kxc2 (the King captures the Queen)
- **Qd3+** loses immediately to Kxd3 (the King captures the Queen, and d3 is undefended)
- **Qb3+** loses immediately to Kxb3
- **Qxb2+** loses to Kxb2 (trading Queen for Knight is losing)

The only safe checking move is **Qc1+**, which attacks the King along the c-file. From c1:
- The King cannot capture (too far)
- The King cannot go to c2, c4, or d2 (attacked by the Queen)
- The King cannot go to d3 or c4 (controlled by the Knight on b2, and c4 also by the Queen)
- The King must flee to b3, b4, or d4

**Qc1+** is the most forcing move that maintains Black's winning advantage while keeping the attack going.

make_move b1c1

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at this endgame position, I need to assess the material and threats carefully:

**Material count:**
- Black: King + 2 pawns
- White: King + Knight + 1 pawn

White has a material advantage (Knight ≈ 3 pawns, so White is effectively up about 2 pawns). This means I need to play actively to create counterplay or simplify toward a drawable ending.

**Immediate threats:**
White threatens Nd1 followed by Nf2+, which would trap my king in the h1-corner. I need to move my king out of the corner to avoid this tactical motif.

**King moves analysis:**
- **h1h2**: Keeps the king passive on the edge
- **h1g1**: Gets off the corner but remains somewhat passive
- **h1g2**: Centralizes the king actively, controls more squares (f1, f2, f3, g1, g3, h1, h2, h3), and prevents Nd1-Nf2+ tricks since the king vacates h1

Given that I need to activate my pieces to fight for counterplay with the pawns on the queenside, centralizing the king to g2 is the most principled move.

make_move h1g2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at this endgame position, I can see:
- Black has a king on g2, pawns on a7 and b4
- White has a king on c1 and a knight on c5

The pawns are currently blocked by White's knight. If I advance a7-a6 or a7-a5, the knight can simply capture on a6 (Nxa6). Similarly, if I advance the b-pawn, the knight can capture it.

However, the b-pawn is already on the 4th rank (b4), making it more advanced and valuable as a passed pawn. By pushing it to b3, I create immediate threats:
1. If White captures with Nxb3, I can then advance my a-pawn to create counterplay on the queenside
2. If White ignores it, I continue marching toward promotion

Moving the king first (like Kf3 or Kf1) allows White to capture the b-pawn anyway, so it's better to push the b-pawn immediately while it's still on the board.

make_move b4b3

After analyzing the legal moves, I need to choose a move that maintains Black's central advantage and prepares active counterplay. The move **c7c5** is excellent here because:

1. It challenges White's control of the center and prepares to open lines for the queen and light-squared bishop
2. It creates a strong queenside pawn majority and potential pressure along the c-file
3. It prevents White from easily achieving d4, which would challenge Black's central pawns
4. If White does play d4, Black can respond with ...cxd4 creating isolated pawn structures or favorable exchanges

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at the position, I can see that White has just developed their knights to a3 and f3, and played c3/e3/h3, creating a somewhat passive setup. Black has a powerful centralized knight on e4, supported by the d5 pawn.

I spot a tactical opportunity: the knight on e4 can jump to f2 (e4f2), forking both the White queen on d1 and the rook on h1! From f2, the knight attacks:
- d1 (the Queen)
- h1 (the Rook) 
- d3 and h3 as well

After **1...Nxf2**, White has no choice but to capture with **2.Kxf2** (since moving the king away loses the queen to Nxd1, and no other piece can capture the knight). This wins a pawn, exposes White's king, and forces it to move to an awkward square.

This is a decisive tactical blow that wins material and compromises White's king safety.

make_move e4f2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at the board, I can see White's King is at e2 and is already in a dangerous position. Black has a Queen at d4 and a Knight at f2, which creates a powerful mating net.

Let me analyze the key move **Qd3+**:

1. **Queen to d3 gives check** - The Queen moves from d4 to d3, attacking the White King at e2 (adjacent diagonal).

2. **King cannot capture** - The Knight at f2 defends the d3 square, making Kxd3 illegal.

3. **King has no escape squares**:
   - e1 is currently safe, but forced
   - d1 is controlled by the Knight at f2
   - f1 is controlled by the Queen at d3 (diagonal)
   - d2 is occupied by White's own Knight
   - e3 is controlled by the Queen at d3 (horizontal)
   - f2 is occupied by Black's Knight

4. **After Ke1 (only legal move)**, Black plays **Qd1#**:
   - Queen at d1 gives check to King at e1
   - The Knight at f2 defends d1, preventing Kxd1
   - King has no escape squares (d2, e2, f1 all controlled by Queen; f2 occupied; d0 invalid)

This is a classic **smothered mate** pattern!

make_move d4d3

- The King cannot capture the new Queen on d1 because that square is protected by the Black Bishop on g4

- The King cannot flee to f2 (attacked by both the Queen on d1 and the Knight on e4)

- No other White piece can capture or block the Queen on d1

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at this position, Black has a significant advantage with the queen actively placed at e4 and a dangerous passed pawn at b3. The king is currently in the center, so securing it should be a priority.

Let me analyze the key options:

**Queenside Castling (e8c8)**: This is compelling because:
- It moves the king to safety on c8 (note: c8 isn't attacked because the g4 bishop blocks the h3-bishop's diagonal)
- It brings the a8 rook to d8, activating it on the open d-file and attacking the d2 pawn
- It connects the rooks

**Pawn push (b3b2)**: Tempting since b3 is a passed pawn, but after b3b2, White simply captures with c3xb2 or Bxb2, and the pawn is lost without sufficient compensation.

**Queen moves**: While the queen is active at e4, many captures (like Qxf4 or Qxh4) lose the queen to pawn recaptures. Qb1+ loses the queen for a rook after Rxb1.

**e5xf4**: This opens the e-file but exposes the queen to attack from the e1 rook after the queen moves.

**Conclusion**: Castling queenside is the most principled move, securing the king and activating the rook while maintaining all threats.

make_move e8c8

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at this board, I can see Black has a massive attack against the exposed White King on e4. Black has two queens (at d2 and h5), a rook on d7, and other pieces in active positions.

Let me verify the final blow: The queen on d2 can move to f4, capturing the pawn there and delivering checkmate.

**Analysis of Qxf4#:**
- The queen moves from d2 to f4 along the diagonal, capturing the f4 pawn
- This delivers check to the White King at e4
- The queen is protected by the pawn on e5, so the king cannot capture
- The king cannot flee:
  - d3, d4, d5 are controlled by the rook on d7
  - e3, f3 are controlled by the checking queen on f4
  - e5 is occupied by a black pawn
  - f5 is controlled by both the checking queen and the queen on h5
- No White piece can block the diagonal check at e3
- No White piece can capture the queen at f4 (the knight at b1 is too far away)

This is checkmate!

make_move d2f4

Let me analyze the forcing checks:

**Key observation:** The pawn on e5 blocks the Queen's horizontal movement to the left (d5, c5, etc.), and the White King on e4 is stalemated in many directions by the Black Rook on d7 and Knight on c6.

The most forcing move is **Rd4+**:
- The Rook moves from d7 to d4, giving check to the King on e4
- The King cannot capture on d4 (illegal, as d4 is defended by the Knight on c6)
- The King cannot move to d3 or d5 (attacked by the Rook on d7 along the file)
- The only legal move is **Ke3**

After **Rd4+ Ke3**, Black has a continuing attack with moves like Qh4 or Qd1, keeping the pressure on. This check essentially traps the King in a box and prepares for a quick checkmate.

While other checks like Qh4+ or Qd1+ are possible, they allow the King more escape squares (f3, f5). Rd4+ is the most restrictive and professional choice.

5. No White pieces can capture the Queen on f5

The key weakness in White's position is the bishop at c1, which is currently defended only by the knight at e2. Black can eliminate this crucial defender with tempo:

**make_move g4e2**

- If **2.Qxe2**, then Black's knight on e4 remains loose and will be captured by the queen (2...Nxe4 is impossible as the knight is undefended and hangs), but actually after the queen recaptures on e2, Black can save the knight or play **2...Qxc1** (since the defender is gone), winning the c1 bishop. Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at this position, I need to assess the tactical opportunities. Black has very active pieces - the queen on e5, bishop on e2, and knights on d6 and e7 are all aggressively placed against White's king stuck on h1.

The key tactical motif here is **Nde4** (knight from d6 to e4). This move:
1. Centralizes the knight powerfully
2. Threatens **Nxf2#** (smothered mate), since White's king on h1 is blocked by its own rook on h2 and pawn on g2
3. Cannot be captured (nothing attacks e4)
4. Can only be stopped by 2.Rf2, losing the exchange

After 1...Nde4:
- If 2.Rf2 Nxf2+ 3.Bxf2, Black has won a rook for a knight
- If White tries anything else, Nxf2 is checkmate

Other tries like ...Qf4 run into 2.exf4, and ...Bxc4+ is good but not as decisive.

**make_move d6e4**

Player_Black (to Proxy):

I see the position now. The White bishop on g5 is checking my king on d7. Let me analyze my options:

1. **Ke8** (d7e8) - Moves the king to safety behind the pawn wall
2. **Kd8** (d7d8) - Moves the king back but keeps it more exposed
3. **Kd6** (d7d6) - Advances the king but it's still in the open
4. **Kc6** (d7c6) - Sideways move, potentially risky
5. **Nf5** (e7f5) or **Nxf5** (f7f5) - Knight moves that block the check

The knight on e7 moving to f5 (e7f5) would block the check and attack the bishop, but after ...Nf5 Bxd7 Nxd7, White has strong pressure.

The safest move is **Ke8** (d7e8), tucking the king away safely behind the pawn on e7 and getting out of the check immediately.

make_move d7e8

Looking at this position, I can see Black has a decisive attack with the Queen deep in White's territory on c1, while White's King is exposed on g2.

The winning move is **Qb2+** (c1b2 in UCI notation), delivering a check that leads to forced mate:

- If **2. Kf1**, then **2...Qf2#** (Queen is protected by the Knight on e4, and the King has no escape squares).
- If **2. Kh1**, then **2...Qg2#** (immediate mate).
- If **2. Nc2** (blocking), then **2...Qxc2+** and mate follows shortly (3...Qg2# or 3...Qf2# depending on White's response).
- Other King moves like 2. Kf3 or 2. Kh3 allow Black to maintain a decisive attack with moves like Qf2+.

This is a classic example of a Queen + Knight mating net against a castled King.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at the board, I can see that Black has a powerful attack with the queen on b2 deeply positioned in White's territory. The white king is on g2, and there's a white bishop on e2 blocking the queen's line of attack.

The most forcing move is **Qxe2+** (queen captures the bishop on e2 with check). This move:
1. Wins a piece (the bishop on e2)
2. Gives check to the white king on g2
3. Forces the king to move (since no piece can capture the queen on e2 and the line cannot be blocked)

After 1...Qxe2+ 2.Kg1 (or Kh1), Black can continue with checks like Qe1+ or other aggressive moves to maintain the initiative.

**make_move b2e2**

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at this position, I have a dominant attacking position with my queen deep inside White's territory on e2. Let me analyze the immediate threats.

The White king is on g1, and my queen on e2 is positioned to deliver a lethal blow. If I play **Qe1+**, this puts the king in check along the first rank (e1-f1-g1). 

Let me verify if this is checkmate:
- The king cannot capture the queen (two squares away on e1)
- The king cannot move to f1 (attacked by the queen)
- The king cannot move to h1 (attacked by the queen along the first rank)
- The king cannot move to f2 (attacked by the queen on e1 diagonally)
- The king cannot move to h2 (occupied by White's own rook)
- The check cannot be blocked (no White piece can reach f1 to interpose)
- The queen cannot be captured by any White piece

This is checkmate!

make_move e2e1

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Looking at this position, I have a powerful attack with my queen at e1 and knight at e4 against White's king at g2. The white pieces are poorly coordinated, with the king trapped on the back rank and the rook at h2 unable to defend effectively.

I see a forced checkmate sequence starting with a queen sacrifice:

**1...Qxg3+!** (e1g3)
If 2.Kh1 (forced, since 2.Kf1 loses to Qf2# and 2.Kh3 loses to Qh2#)
**2...Nf2+!** 
If 3.Rxf2 (only legal move)
**3...Qxf2#**

The queen at f2 delivers checkmate - the king at h1 cannot capture (knight-move away), cannot move to g1 (controlled by queen), and cannot move to h2 (controlled by queen).

Even if White tries 3.Kg1 instead of capturing the knight, Black still wins the rook and has a decisive attack.

This is a beautiful forced mate in 3!

make_move e1g3
